B0033AX20E|5.0|This is the beginning of DragonForce (minus the demo they did when they were called DragonHeart) and to tell the truth, this is WAY, WAY better than Inhuman Rampage! ZP Theart is very confident in his voice and sounds like this is his billionth time recording when this is really one of his 1st times. Sam and Herman...what can I say that hasn't been said? These two are monster guitar players and as always, are kickin some major hindquarters during the album. This album has a different drummer than the later albums (but at this speed, who can tell the difference?), Didier Almouzni is a very good drummer but this will be his only outing with the 'Force. Dave Mackintosh takes over after this album but both are crazy, but amazing drummers. I personally like Dave better because the fills in later albums are much more intense. Vadim is a boss as usual. He's just one of the best keyboardists around today, with Jordan Rudess, Michael Pinnella, and Jens Johansson. This album is probably my favorite album from these guys so far and I think this is best cover art they've ever had!<br /><br />On to the music!<br /><br />1. \\"Invocation of Apocalyptic Evil\\"- Just an intro to the title track...nothing special.<br /><br />2. \\"Valley of the Damned\\"- A very energetic track! This is perhaps their most epic song (excluding the mighty \\"My Spirit Will Go On\\"). The guitars in this one aren't over the top/too busy like some of their later albums. They have nice riffing and melodies on this one. Props to ZP on this one! I love the way he sings this one and this one is probably the one I like singing along to the most (save for \\"Black Winter Night\\"). Great anthem from these guys!<br /><br />3. \\"Black Fire\\"- Very intense riffing on the intro and the intensity only continues throughout the song. ZP's vocals get more emotional and passionate even with all the speed! This chorus is one of the best ones on this album! I love this song because even though some of their songs might blend together due to their speed, this one has a unique tone and quality to it that makes it identifiable. The breakdown in this one is pretty intense. Very good tune here as well.<br /><br />4. \\"Black Winter Night\\"- One of my all time favorite DragonForce songs! The intro already sounds cool, but the song gets even better! I heard this version of it first an thought it was pretty dang awesome! But I recently looked up the demo version of this one because of Brandon B.'s review. I have to say in terms of this song, I agree that this chorus sucks compared to the demo version! I like this song regardless, but I think it had a MUCH better chorus on the demo version. I also think though that the prechorus would make a much better chorus than either of the two different choruses. The part \\"On the endless seas of madness...\\" would be the best chorus. But oh well...This song is just great no matter how you look at it and it is one of my absolute favorite songs by this band. But for the record, the demo version is better. :p<br /><br />5. \\"Starfire\\"- This has to be their best ballad. I've heard two of their other ballads and well...they became a little too guitar oriented. This one is nice and piano driven with the guitars coming in for some solos here and there, but this one flows better than their later ballads. ZP and Vadim are the stars of this song and they really shine brightly here. The acoustic guitar solos are also very nice. Very, very nice song here, guys!<br /><br />6. \\"Disciples of Babylon\\"- As far as DragonForce goes, this is one of their most unique songs. While you'll probably think is sounds normal at the beginning, the middle where all the solos usually are is what makes this one unique. The metal band fades out and then a beautiful passage with acoustic guitar, piano solo, and drumming comes in instead of the rapid fire solos we normally get. This instrumental sounds more progressive as it moves around into different grooves, has softer parts, the electric guitar eventually comes in, and yeah, this song is just very unique in the solo part and it's more original than the rest of their songs. Great song!<br /><br />7. \\"Revelations\\"- this song is filler. It starts off unique, but it turns into a song that is WAY TOO similar to the title track. filler.<br /><br />8. \\"Evening Star\\"- This song starts off slow with ZP and Vadim. And then Herman and Sam and the crew come in and do some some fast-paced metal stuff. The 1st riff in here sounds like \\"My Spirit Will Go On\\" but on that note, not even close to being as cool as that song! This song is pretty cool during the chorus and a few riffs, but really, it's not an essential song from here. I like it a lot tho.<br /><br />9. \\"Heart of a Dragon\\"- This gets the award for cheesiest song they've done...it's really not that good. I think it's one of the biggest let downs because this album is filled with great things (except Revelations) and this is just BAD. I don't like this one at all.<br /><br />10. \\"Where Dragons Rule\\"- After hearing the previous song with the word \\"Dragon\\" in the name, I thought this would be Cheese fest part 2. Actually, this song is good! I love how it starts with Vadim. I love the piano intro and how it progresses and unfolds into another fast song. This one has a pretty good chorus even if it is about dragons haha. I'm satisfied with this bonus track. This one ends the album right. If the previous track ended it, I'd be unhappy because that'd be a terrible ending to an amazing album, but this one sets it right.<br /><br />Verdict: This is probably the best DragonForce album (If this isn't the best, then I think Sonic Firestorm would be the best. I haven't heard that one yet, but I will). I recommend this to any fan of DragonForce, HammerFall, Stratovarius, and other power metal bands. If you've heard DragonForce before, buy this album. If you haven't heard them before, start here! If you like super fast speed metal, these guys will not disappoint! These guys are someof the most talented musicians out there. I suggest picking up this album before any other one.<br /><br />also: I've heard all of this and \\"Inhuman Rampage\\", about half of \\"The Power Within\\", some of \\"Ultra Beatdown\\" and one song; \\"My Spirit Will Go On\\" from \\"Sonic Firestorm\\".<br /><br />here's my listing:<br />best: \\"Valley of the Damned\\"<br />\\"The Power Within\\"<br />\\"Inhuman Rampage\\"<br />\\"Ultra Beatdown\\"<br /><br />I didn't put \\"Sonic\\" in there because I've only heard one song. But yes, this is my honest opinion so far. This one is their best and I suggest buying this one!

Throughout his life he inspired scores of people, and his story has continued to change the lives of many -including mine. B007C3TVNA|2.0|We Need To Talk About Kevin was one of the most disturbingly fascinating books I've ever read. I finally got to see it on DVD and was left terribly disappointed.<br /><br />To be fair, I don't believe anyone could have done a film version justice. The book told the story entirely from inside Eva's head, and the reader viewed Kevin with the same confusion as she did. We wondered along with her, was Eva a horrible mother? Or was Kevin just \\"born bad\\"? Each incident could be viewed from both sides of the equation as Eva turned it over in her mind, struggling to comprehend what led up to the school massacre.<br /><br />We can't get inside Eva's head in a film, and can only witness her interactions with her son as outsiders. The complexity of their relationship is lost. We see Kevin as a bratty child and a sullen teenager, but honestly not all that different from a lot of other kids. It's therefore harder to empathize with Eva, and she comes off as a one-dimensional, resentful woman who didn't want a child and doesn't like the one she got. No wonder Kevin is so cold to her, and gravitates towards his father! The book showed far more depth to both characters' personalities.<br /><br />The epistolary format of the book was something that could not have been easily translated to the screen, but it was a big part of what made the book so breathtaking, particularly at the end, when we realize why Eva is now estranged from her husband. The portrayal of Franklin, by the way, is one of the few things the film improved on. John C. Reilly made Eva's husband far more likable than the clueless dolt he was in the book, refusing to believe that his darling son could do wrong. I can not say the same for Tilda Swinton as Eva. I'm surprised at the rave reviews she received - I found her completely bland and emotionless.<br /><br />Finally, I was disappointed by the glossing over of the actual massacre in the school gym. Not that I was looking forward to the gore, but Kevin's meticulous,finely-detailed planning of the \\"big event\\" in the book added to the horror and showed us what a twisted soul he really was.<br /><br />The novel was a five star read. It really should have been left alone.

B0071XO8RA|4.0|In a bleak, post-apocalyptic future, what's left of humanity resides in an enormous underground silo. Alas, all is not peaceful within the safety of the silo (of course). While most of the silo's inhabitants go about their lives blissfully accepting of their fate, a few begin to question...<br /><br />Sound familiar? Sure... this book does mirror the premise of several survival stories (and underground survival stories at that) that have come before. But then again, I have yet to see many truly new premises that I've never seen before. That said, this book takes some delightfully creative twists and turns from this basic idea and kept me guessing most of the way through.<br /><br />This novel consists of 5 &#34;books&#34; - although in my opinion only the first is capable of standing on it's own as a compelling short story or novelette (though it's a bit too short to be called that). I do think that this first story is well done - I had actually purchased it before I purchased this collection in order to get deeper into the story. The remaining 4 really should be sections within a larger book. The book explores the mysteries of the silo, its inhabitants and power struggles, and the larger issue of what happened to humanity to bring about this bleak condition. I feared, when I began reading, that the book would become mired in the minutia of silo politics and infighting but, to my relief, it spends more time exploring this &#34;world&#34; and uncovering its many mysteries without getting bogged down like a bad futuristic soap opera. The characters are well written, the story compelling and although you may guess at some of the plot twists before they occur (a favorite game of mine) you probably won't see many of them coming.<br /><br />I did find the romance between two key characters a bit thin and took issue with the logic of the event that starts it all off, but these are relatively small quibbles that don't detract too much from what is a well written, creative and suspenseful story. I'm particularly impressed with the quality of the writing from a relatively unknown and (I believe?) unsigned author. B00866H7SS|5.0|Absolutely could not put it down. A story that just grabs you from the first few pages and does not let you think about anything else even days after you finished reading it.<br /><br />The thing that struck me the most about this book were the dialogues between Eva and Gideon - it was so refreshing to have a heroine who was not afraid to speak up her mind, regardless of her feelings for this out-of-this-world man. I just loved how she keeps him on his toes throughout the book, never quite allows him to take her for granted. The love scenes were, needless to say, beyond hot but also necessary to make the reader fully understand the nature of the need that these two characters develop for each other. It is not just carnal, a book like that would not keep you glued to the pages so well, it is purely psychological. Eva and Gideon are both \\"damaged\\" individuals due to very unfortunate pasts. They were both abused as children but they cope with the damage of those experiences quite differently. Eva has been in therapy ever since her mother found out about the abuse and she has dealt with her demons. She has grown into a strong-willed and confident young woman who still admits having low self-esteem issues but I think she's doing quite well considering her past. Gideon, on the other hand, has just swept it all under a rug and uses control in all aspects of his life to deal with his issues. Not having control over Eva is what initially attracts him the most to her, in my opinion. For the first time in his life he has to let his guard down in order to get what he craves the most - Eva's affection and acceptance. She does not judge him, she keeps no secrets from him and tells him exactly what she is thinking at any given moment, never afraid to hurt his feelings with the truth. He has to work to keep her and that is something he has never had to do with another woman. He falls for her deeply and starts opening up. His vulnerability to her is just beautiful to see. He does not accept losing her, ever, cannot see it as an option at all. He's upset when she refers to their relationship as \\"temporary\\" and is not afraid to express the depth of his feelings towards her for the whole world to see. The chemistry between them is undeniable but it is evident that their physical connection is something they both also desperately need to fight their demons and feel safe in each other's arms. They are literally each other's therapy.<br /><br />This is a book that took my heart on such a roller-coaster ride that I had to stop at times to catch my breath. I felt their pain, I felt their anger and I felt the intensity of their love for each other. What more can you ask of a book?<br /><br />Any similarity to Fifty Shades of Grey is truly coincidental. I guess both Christian Grey and Gideon Cross are strong alpha males with heavy emotional baggage and dominant personalities but they deal with their issues somewhat differently and I could not draw too many parallels at all. The female characters have nothing in common with each other. Eva is strong from the start, Ana has to learn to find her voice. It makes me mad when people compare the two stories implying that one inspired the other. They are such different pieces of writing and both beautiful and compelling in their own rights. My only criticism of Bared to You is that I now have to wait until October 2012 to see how this story develops even further. B0058S0OMS|4.0|I remember it as vividly as any memory of my youth - I was about 7 years old. I woke up in the middle of the night and went to the kitchen for a glass of water. Instead of drinking it and going directly to bed I sat down with my drink and turned on the TV. It was HBO and a movie was coming on; little did I know it would be Halloween II and that I would remember sitting and watching that movie for the rest of my life. It has been nearly 30 years since that night, but I remember having nightmares about it for weeks after seeing it.<br /><br />Now that Im an adult and have come to appreciate a good scare, I look back on my first encounter with \\"The Shape\\" very fondly. Although it is not a masterpiece of cinema (unlike it's predecessor) and wont be seen on any AFI lists, \\"Halloween II\\" accomplishes what it sets out to do very well - to create an eerie, claustrophobic, scare-a-thon, that leaves its audience gripping the armwrests of their theater seats until the thrilling, climatic conclusion.<br /><br />A short synopsis would be this: Laurie Strode survives her first encounter with Michael Myers and is transported to Haddonfield Memorial Hospital where she must endure one more epic struggle with the immortal serial killer in the dark corridors of the nearly abandoned hospital before her nightmare will finally come to an end.<br /><br />'Halloween II' is not a methodically paced, psychological thriller; as opposed to a true horror film like the original John Carpenter classic - it is, in fact, almost the antithesis to the original with lots of blood and gore, but therein lies the genius of the film. Where the original played to frighten you psychologically by lurking in the darkness, the 2nd film tries to scare the hell out of you with everything up close and personal, and it succeeds brilliantly. B000006UD8|5.0|This 1972 release by English heavy metal group Deep Purple is thunderous; a massive wall of progressive hard rock that overwhelms at times.  Although I have not listened to this record in a long (long) time, the memories came back pretty quickly and it is every bit as good as I remember it being.  Better even, because there no skips and pops with the CD format.<br /><br />For me, the Mk. II lineup defined the Deep Purple sound; the ensemble playing and group dynamic that they established on Machine Head is awesome.  Roger Glover (love the tone he gets out of his Rickenbacker bass) and drummer Ian Paice burn on this album and Ritchie Blackmore proves himself a virtuoso on the electric guitar.  I am a huge fan of Jon Lord's Hammond organ playing - he runs it through a fuzz box (I think) which gives his organ a simply ferocious sound - the classical flourishes are also nice.  Last but certainly not least, is lead singer (screamer) Ian Gillan.  This guy has one of the most powerful voices in rock (then and now); a voice that occupies a space somewhere between Peter Hammill and Arthur Brown.<br /><br />Although Highway Star and (especially) Smoke on the Water suffered from overplaying on the radio, the album still sounds fresh today - the sound on Machine Head is not dated in the least.  Many of the tracks are built around (awesome) riffs - the guys vary the riffs though, so they do not simply lapse into an endless ostinato over which solos are layered.  There is a somewhat improvisational feel to certain of the tracks - this would be explored further on the live album Made in Japan (1972). My favorite tracks include Pictures of Home and the bluesy track Lazy - this is a great tune to jam to.<br /><br />This remastered album is great and features liner notes by Roger Glover, a few extra tracks (singles), and various remixes of the album (one by Roger in 1997).  The 1997 remix sounds like a \\"live in the studio\\" recording - the remix of Lazy is excellent.  There are also quadrophonic mixes of a few tunes.  I have to admit that I am a stick in the mud when it comes to these things and really have no interest in the extra tracks (I have the vinyl album burned into my mind).  However, they should prove of interest to fans.  The sound quality of the original remastered album is outstanding.<br /><br />All in all, this is a fantastic album of hard rock that boasts the definitive Deep Purple sound.  Highly recommended along with the blistering In Rock (1970), the more experimental Fireball (1971), and one of the landmark live rock albums Made in Japan (1972).uadrophonic mixes of a few tunes. 0345525558|5.0|The Language of Flowers, a selection of the Riverfront Book Club, is a realistic commentary on the foster care system in America that often leaves children emotionally scarred, angry and bitter. In alternating chapters of past and present, the reader meets Victoria, recently emancipated from foster care, homeless and desperately seeking her place in a world that seems hostile and empty. Victoria is rescued by Renata, a florist with emotional issues of her own, and through their relationship, both come to a new understanding of what it means to be part of a family. This is a story about forgiveness, second chances and the power of love as its characters Victoria, Elizabeth, Renata and Grant learn to express their emotions in ways that foster self healing, emotional intimacy and finally, trust and self-acceptance. Victoria learns that she is not the only human being that is flawed. Once she accepts responsiblity for her years of violence and self-destructive behavior, she begins to understand how to create a new and healthy reality for herself and those she begins to love. At the end of the novel, Victoria learns that moss grows without roots--that love is imperfect and can grow anywhere when one's heart is open.<br /><br />The descriptions and details of the flowers and the techniques of florist work provide beauty and solace in comparison to the painful realities of the character's lives.<br /><br />The ending is one of hope and reconciliation.
B007G92QGO|4.0|In her novel 'We Need to Talk About Kevin', Lionel Shriver gave us a 'fundamentally evil' child and an obnoxiously self-centred unreliable narrator in his mother.  In 'Defending Jacob', Landay approaches similar territory, but with a more fascinatingly complex view on the narrator character and his wife, something a lot closer to sympathy for the fourteen-year-old boy - and a tense courtroom drama as well.<br /><br />This is a pacy, taut, twisting tale that examines Jacob himself in leisurely, almost laconic detail.  His father, the sometimes-sharp-as-a-tack narrator, has his own secrets, blind spots and failings, which we intermittently see more or less clearly.  His mother, similarly, drifts in and out of the zone of our empathy: the picture of her fluctuates between that of an almost standard over-emotionally self-righteous wife character and something a lot more complicated and off-beam. No one is 'right', even at the end, and this is one of the many ways in which 'Defending Jacob' is good.<br /><br />The courtroom set-pieces are expertly and knowledgeably dramatised, with shifting characterisations that push us way out into a sea of uncertainty and tension, excited about the outcome but really not sure what side we ought to be on.<br /><br />It's very well worth a read (and a much better book than 'Kevin').

0871404133|1.0|1.0 out of 5 stars A HERO FALLS FROM GRACE, June 4, 2012<br /><br />By Featherless Biped - See all my reviews<br /><br />Amazon Verified Purchase(What's this?)<br />This review is from: The Social Conquest of Earth (Hardcover)<br /><br />Wilson has always been one of my heros--not only an outstanding biologist, but one of the tiny and vanishing minority of intellectuals who at least dares to hint at the truth others studiously avoid.<br /><br />I found sections with the usual incisive commentary (though nothing really new or interesting if you have read his other works and are up on biology in general) in the often stilted prose that is his hallmark, but was quite surprised that the core of the book is his rejection of inclusive fitness (which has been a mainstay of evolutionary biology for over 40 years) in favor of group selection. One assumes that coming from him and published in major peer reviewed journals like Nature, it must be a substantial advance in spite of the fact that I knew group selection had always been nearly universally rejected due to its basic conflict with our understanding of evolutionary biology.<br /><br />I have read all the reviews here and on the net and many have good comments but the one I most wanted to see was that by renowned science writer and evolutionary biologist<br />Richard Dawkins. Unlike most of those by professionals, which are in journals only available to those with access to a university, it is readily available on the net.<br /><br />Sadly one finds a devastating rejection of the book and some of the most trenchant commentary on a scientific colleague I have ever seen from Dawkins--exceeding anything I recall<br />even in his many exchanges with late and unlamented demagogue and pseudoscientist Stephan Jay Gould. Although Gould was infamous for his personal attacks on his Harvard colleague<br />Wilson, Dawkins notes that much of this book reminds one uncomfortably of Goulds frequent lapses into \\"bland, unfocussed ecumenicalism\\".<br /><br />Dawkins points out that Wilson's 2010 paper in Nature was almost universally rejected by over 140 biologists who responded with letters and that there is not one word about this in<br />Wilson's book. Nor does Wilson correct this in his public lectures. There is no choice but to agree with Dawkin's trenchant comment \\"For Wilson not to acknowledge that he speaks for himself against the great majority of his professional colleagues is--it pains me to say this of a lifelong hero --an act of wanton arrogance.\\" I feel like one of the stunned people one sees on TV being interviewed after the nice man next door, who has been babysitting everyone's children for 30 years, is exposed as a serial killer.<br /><br />Dawkins also points out (once again) that inclusive fitness is entailed by (i.e.,logically follows from) neodarwinism and cannot be rejected without rejecting evolution itself. Wilson again reminds us of Gould, who denounced creationists from one side of his mouth while giving them comfort by spewing endless ultraliberal marxist tinged gibberish about spandrels,punctuated equilibrium and evolutionary psychology from the other. The vagueness of group and multilevel selection is just what the softminded want to enable them to escape<br />rational thinking in their endless antiscientific postmodernist word salads.<br /><br />It is rare that scientists responding to devastating criticism actually admit their mistakes and Wilson and his Harvard math colleages, who wrote the now infamous trash paper in the famous journal Nature in 2010 (you can also do yourself a favor by avoiding Martin Nowak's books),are no exception, failing to respond in any meaningful way in their replies.<br /><br />Worse yet, Wilson's book is a poorly thought out and sloppily written mess full of nonsequiturs, vague ramblings, confusions and incoherence. A good review that details some of these is that by graduate student Gerry Carter which you can find on the net. Wilson is also out of touch with our current understanding of evolutionary psychology (EP)(see e.g., the last 300 pages of Pinker's \\"The Better Angels of our Nature\\"). If you want a serious account of social evolution and some relevant EP from a<br />biological standpoint see Principles of Social Evolution by Andrew F.G. Bourke,or a not quite so serious and admittedly flawed and rambling account but a must read nevertheless by Robert Trivers--The Folly of Fools: The Logic of Deceit and Self-Deception in Human Life and older but still current and penetrating works such as The Evolution of Cooperation: Revised Edition by Robert Axelrod and The Biology of Moral Systems by Richard Alexander.<br /><br />I see no point in repeating others comments so I will end with a remark I recall reading a half century ago--I think by the famous philosopher Bertrand Russell--<br />that one can find even in the best minds a \\"nest of furry caterpillars\\". The main reason, I guess, is I enjoy YA fiction (JK Rowling, Philip Pullman) and, since I'm a very amateur writer myself, I decided it was worth checking out the Twilight books to find a key on how to write a good, compelling story in this genre, may it be about vampires or not. I wanted to learn from it, honestly. I did learn, but not in the way I was hoping I would.<br />I agree with 99 per cent of what's being said in other reviews about Bella's character. I don't think it's flat, just plain unlikeable. I did relate to her in the first part of the first book, moving from home, facing a new school and new people, with her anxiety and so on. But then her way of handling her relationships with friends and family and her totally passive obsession with Edward put me off it completely.<br />I had been expecting vampires to be mysterious creatures, dangerous and tormented, not a bunch of Beverly Hills kids with tons of money and clothes and whatever else. Or, even so, that this superficiality would be treated as such, not as something to be pursued (beauty, money and so on), but as values that do not in themselves grant happiness. Which in this story they do, since Bella gets all of that. I agree with her not deserving the perfectly wrapped up happy ending.<br />I also agree with most of the ethical issues that have been raised about mysogyny, paedophilia, domestic abuse, lobotomy (ie imprinting) and so on. I found them disturbing, but I would have tolerated their presence had they been delved more deeply into and shown their negative sides, had theybeen thought-provoking, but this doesn't seem to be the case.<br />However, I'll try to be true to Oscar Wilde when he says that books are either well-written or not but they are not meant to be judged on their underlying morals, whether they be present or not. Aside from that, then, the books have never worked for me, for two main reasons.<br />The first reason is that they have no plot, no substance, no purpose. SM has said it herself that the concept of her story stems from a dream she had. Nothing wrong with that, but it shows very explicitly and the inspiration from the dream culminates in chapter 13 of Twilight. She built up the story to get there. We may like the way she did it or not, but that's where she was headed.<br />After that climax, the whole thing began to go downwards, because, from my point of view, SM herself didn't know what to do with the story or her characters anymore. They had no purpose, nothing specific to do. They had no real war to fight, nothing or no one to find or rescue, no place to go, no mission to accomplish (except for Bella to become a vampire, which I thought was ludicrous from the start. I did expect her to become one, but not to be obsessed by the idea, rather to be forced into it by necessity; in a way that's how it was, but I found this to be, as the main storyline, quite poor and against logic). The story just develops around the relationship Bella/Edward, which is established half way through the first book and is never really in danger since.<br />This is the first big problem in the book. Episodes follow each other, quite slowly, including a lot of cheesy dialog (most of Twilight was made of questions and answers: today is my turn to ask questions, tomorrow is your turn. Come on, what kind of suspense is that?), without a specific drive. There are plenty of characters, many of whom quite interesting. They would have made a good story if only they'd had something to actually \\"do\\". The flashbacks about their past were intriguing, but they were placed here or there at random without any action justifying their being brought up. I remember Rosalie's. She and Bella just sit on the bed: let me tell you about my story and so on. It would have been so much more interesting if this flashback had been introduced in a moment when Rosalie's life had been at stake, or she had done something dramatic or had disappeared or something to that effect and the Cullens had justified her behavior by bringing up her past. Ninety per cent of the books are based on talking, smooching, throwing parties, picking dresses. This doesn't work for me, not when I'm pushing forty, but it wouldn't have worked at sixteen either.<br />Every volume is built in much the same way: a prologue trying to capture the reader's attention with the foreshadowing of some kind of dramatic event, which proves to be not that dramatic, a long section of talking, romance, teen stuff that has no real relevance except to a very young audience, a final part with some action to justify the existence of the book itself, action which is often not described because Bella isn't in it or because it is resolved let's say \\"diplomatically\\" (again, talking).<br />This gives me the opportunity to explore the other main problem the book has, which SM herself, IMO, realized, albeit a little late: the use of the first person is great in that it gets you more deeply involved with the character and its predicaments, but it shuts you out of what other characters do and in this series, since Bella is the least active of all the characters both because of her personality and for her being an ordinary mortal, this happens in all the crucial moments. This is why awkward devices are introduced in order to be able to show some scenes that we otherwise would not be able to see, being stuck with Bella, such as a showdown between Edward and Jacob. We get to see them arguing in the tent but only because Bella is in the sleeping bag with Jacob pretending to be asleep. How believable is that (I loved someone's remark about an electric blanket)? And how believable is it that a guy who tampers with a girl's car to prevent her from going to see his rival would then take her cheating sitting down (the kiss between Jacob and Bella in Eclispse) and even suggest she has kids with said rival (in Breaking Dawn)?<br />As I said, I think SM realized this herself, since she turns over to Jacob's POV in the last bit of Eclipse and in a full book in Breaking Dawn. How else would she have been able to show the whole impriting process, all of Jacob's internal struggle, his relationship with the pack and so on? Impossible to do through Bella's first person POV. I actually appreciated this part, and I think SM herself enjoyed the change, but it was inconsistent with the writing strategy used throughout.<br />Overall, there were some parts of the books I liked and mostly they were the ones not concerning the romantic highschool storyline, but Jacob's discovering his real nature and all the part about the wolves and the pack and some flashbacks about how the other characters got to be vampires. I also think that SM did a pretty good job of creating the atmosphere and the environment about her characters. But all this was not well played out. The Leah character is compelling and easy to relate to, at least for me, but she's dropped half way through the book so that Jacob can imprint on the baby. I won't even get started on this plot twist or on the baby's name and so on (considering the almost non-existent relationship between Bella and her parents I don't see how she would pick their names for the child anyway).<br />Finally, Breaking Dawn to me as an adult was slightly more exciting (though gruesome and gory) because at least it had some development. Yes, it reads like a horror story, but that's what I thought vampires were supposed to be about all along. Otherwise we could just have been content with the average girl meets highschool hunk, they get together, marry and have kids. Too late to turn to this genre in the end, though, it was not really consistent with the previous tone and with the audience it was meant for. I haven't seen the movie (I haven't seen any of them, to be honest) so I wonder how that was avtually played out.<br />Summing up, the story had a good premise and an interesting concept, but it could have been so much better developed. Instead, it turned out to be a commercial success with very little substance.

0062197258|5.0|This is a very well-written, well-edited book - it's hard to believe it's the author's debut. I found myself engrossed in the story, even though it has two writing devices that I dislike. First, it has a couple of transcripts inserted, which I normally consider a lazy way out of writing a descriptive scene, but they work here because they're used with restraint. Second, the story shifts back and forth in time, which means the reader has to keep adjusting to the narrative. This also works, though, because it creates a low-level of anxiety in the reader which parallels the anxiety in the heroine. Catherine - later Cathy - has good reason to be anxious. Falling for a charming, handsome man, she discovers she's been snared by a sociopath. What is worse than a control freak whose violence escalates? The knowledge that he has disarmed your friends and none of them believe you. It's as if The Terminator has taken over your life, and there's no escape but death. Catherine does her best to escape; laden with emotional baggage, she tries to reconstruct her life and tentatively explore the possibility of a new relationship. But the past catches up to her. After all she's been through, with her emotional fragility, can she stand up to the new terror? Will it ever end? The characters are well drawn and not over-the-top. I did note that there weren't enough variations in dialogue, but it wasn't a major issue. In real life (and in good books) people speak in individual ways, but here all the characters speak alike. Everyone has verbal tics. In a book, if characters don't have different patterns of speech, it can be hard to tell them apart, either literally or by personality. Overall, this is a well-done book and I look forward to reading her next one.

Who hired this guy again?<br /><br />Inept Directing:<br />-There's a scene where a woman who unknowingly had a baby piranha in her vagina is getting intimate with her boyfriend when the piranha comes out and bites his penis. Now this is something that the director and the audience could've had a lot of fun with, they could've played it as really horrifying and/or really funny, but the scene just sort of happens. The woman passes out with puke spurting out of her mouth for some reason and the guy screams and walks over to the kitchen and severs his penis off with the piranha attached. Then right after that chaos everything is quiet and the girl wakes up somehow, walks around until the boyfriend jump-scares her. That's it? It's like they just read the script and filmed it just like that, no visual flair, no funny or ridiculous lines acted, it's all really just bland and trying too hard to be shocking, with no sense of passion from the filmmaker's part.<br />-Another scene earlier has a couple getting kinky in a van using handcuffs when the van rolls into the lake after a mishap with the parking break, the girl can get out but the boyfriend is stuck in the van with the handcuffs still attached, then when he gets attacked you just see a few close-ups of flesh being bitten and see him reacting poorly. You don't really see any of the attack, all you see is another jump-scare where his bloody stump pops open to surprise the girl who's on top of the van. Also when the car is rolling into the water about 1 minute later it's in the middle of the lake, but cars don't float all the way in the middle of a lake before they start sinking, so this was another instance of plot conveniences trumping logic.<br /><br />The Piranha Attack: The disappointment to end all disappointments, Phantom Menace ain't got s*** on this. The shots that establish the park seem like a bunch of advertisements with naked chicks, going tubing down water slides, swimming in the pools, going tubing, etc. When the piranha eventually attack it's those exact same shots again with CGI Piranha added. There's virtually no build-up to the attack, you see one piranha that acts like a scout swim around for a second than swim back into the broken grates to tell the swarm or something. Somehow a solution that equals 'get out of the pool' is stretched out to like 15 minutes of underwhelming mayhem, showing mostly just the heroes randomly running around with some blood and bodies in the pools behind them in the background. The piranha's themselves just weren't threatening either, multiple times characters jump into piranha-infested waters and 'just narrowly' avoid getting eaten by the 5 or 6 piranha, it never feels like a massive swarm of them like in the first one, speaking of the first piranha...<br /><br />What worked for the first Piranha movie's attack was how the scene's worked together. The piranha's swam up to the partiers, bite the first lady, and chaos ensued in many different fun/gross ways. We go from the floating stage being toppled over, to people climbing frantically on boats, to boats running people over in the chaos, to half eaten people crawling out of the water. Here, it's just people splashing in red water that can't get out of the pool, and none of the creative gore such as a woman's hair getting ripped off is evident here, it's just prosthetic bodies. Weak.<br /><br />However there were a few moments where I was cracking up, David Koeschner trying to escape and running over a little kid was pretty great, and Ving Rhames comparing Piranha to kids whizzing in the pool also got a chuckle out of me. Another thing that was pretty funny was the zooming camera shot when Roy Schieder saw the boy get eaten by the shark in Jaws was used repeatedly whenever David Hasselhoff looked around, and the same dramatic shot comes back everytime Hasselhoff turns around again and looks at the carnage; words can't really do that part justice.<br /><br />Those few good chuckles was about all the entertainment value I got from Piranha 3DD though, it's a bad sign when the cameos are the only good thing going for your movie. Piranha 3DD's actual use of the piranhas was lame and underwhelming and overall I wouldn't recommend Piranha 3DD unless you're really curious/bored.ayed it as really horrifying and/or really funny, but the scene just sort of happens. With so many characters to re-introduce and have share in the spotlight, there is no true protagonist who's arc we follow. As a result, with the exception of some really good set pieces, the first two thirds of the film can tend to drag. And the final battle, while well-choreographed and filled with really smart, funny moments (Hulk truly shines in this third act) goes on for too long, and has no rise in tension from the start to the finish. Joss Whedon is an expert storyteller, and he leaps the hurdles that this project came entrenched with deftly. Honestly, no one could do a better job with the material than the man who turned $39million and demand from a small rabid fanbase into the best sci-fi film since Aliens (Serenity, go watch it now if you haven't already). The stage is set for an explosively fun Avengers 2.<br /><br />So I expect to get a fair amount of slams for not gushing more over the movie. I did enjoy it (on second viewing I enjoyed it slightly more than the first time) but this is not a film that I, as a big super-hero/fantasy/sci-fi fan, am ever going to go ga-ga over. I think it did what it needed to do, which was effectively set up the franchise that is to follow, and do so with humor and heart. As the prologue to what is to follow, I think it's top notch. As a standalone film, I think it comes in several ticks below the Nolan Batman flicks (that have their own issues) and the Donner Superman films. However, this is sooo much better than any of the recent X-Men or Fantastic Four movies that it shouldn't even be mentioned along side those lame-duck series.<br /><br />I look forward to the next Avengers movie, and hope that Joss Whedon is still at the helm when it comes.

0525425764|4.0|John Grisham's third Theodore Boone story finds the clever eighth grader attending a trial for a man accused of killing his wife. Theodore gets the last seat at the media-filled event only to learn that the suspect has run away. He marvels that someone could spend his life on the run.<br /><br />On the day of the trial, Theo discovers that his locker has been burglarized, with inhalers, a Twins baseball cap and some notebooks stolen, and later finds his bike tire slashed. After boy scouts the next day, his tire is slashed again. Theo suspects the vandal is another student, but can't think of anyone who hates him that much. He carries his bike to his parents' law offices only to find them in the middle of a big argument among his mothers' divorce clients. He has to leave to go tutor kids at the homeless shelter, so there's no time to tell his parents what has been happening.<br /><br />That night, a computer store is robbed; several expensive laptops, smart phones and more are stolen. At school the following day, Theo is called into the office and asked about the robbery. The detectives search his locker and find two laptops. Theo knows they were not in there that morning and that he did not rob the store. The detectives had received an anonymous tip, though, that Theo was the thief.<br /><br />A photo of Theo and his parents leaving the police station after questioning is sent over the Internet, and soon everyone knows he is accused of the crime. A rock is thrown through the window of his workroom at the law offices, and Theo wants to run away. When his parents make him return to school, he gets in a fight after being taunted and ends up on suspension.<br /><br />The suspension gives Theo time to try and figure out who is targeting him, with the help of his uncle Ike, who likes to bend legal rules to learn information. Through Ike, Theo finds out that he and another family member are actually the targets, and soon the investigation heats up. The police then search the Boone home and make it clear that Theo is their only suspect. Theo's tire is slashed again, and he has to draw on his friends' help and networking to discover who is behind all the attacks.<br /><br />While trying to prove the authorities wrong, Theo has to help with another case in animal court, involving a spitting llama. There is plenty of humor like that in between the attacks on Theo to keep the story moving.<br /><br />THEODORE BOONE: THE ACCUSED is a fast-paced novel with a likable, smart hero and a mystery that is all-too realistic. 0439023513|4.0|I choose to tread this book because I had read the first two in the trilogy. Overall the book was good, but the end was disappointing. This last book about katniss' story, the rebellion and the future of panem is a new journey for katniss and the reader. The theme of rebellion is throughout the whole book. Rebellion is natural to the human race, our history since the very beginning is filled with battle, war, and fighting. It will continue until the end of time.<br /><br />After the bombing in district 8 as katniss is being filmed for a propo, she paints the planes burning on the roof of a warehouse, the capital on a wing glows clearly through the flames and says \\"Fire is catching! And if we burn, you burn with us!(100) This could not have been more true. Not only in the end is the capital destroyed but also are the districts, the rebels and the people of panem. The rebels fight against the capital was not won without loss. From the first book to the third, the mockingjay is symbolic of the rebellion. It starts with Madge, the daughter of district 12's major, giving katniss her mockingjay pin as she says goodbye before katniss goes to the hunger games. Katniss holds up the nightlock berries with peeta, ready to take her own life she triggers the rebellion. In the third nook she becomes the Mockingjay, the face of the rebellion. I think the author chooses this term because of its connection to katniss' past. As a child, her father and her would venture into the woods beyond district 12. Her father would sing and the mockingjays would mimic his voice. Using the term mockingjay reminds the reader and katniss of her father. It is a way to keep his character, memory and spirit alive for both katniss and us. I disagree with how the author ended the book. Like many other people, I thought the ending was rushed. All of collins' books are cliff hangers and leave us wanting to know more. Mockingjay is no different, except it is the last book in the trilogy and there is no more. \\"Mockingjay relentlessly strips aside those feelings of faith and hope...\\" I think the author does this so we feel like Katniss \\"shattered, empty and drained.\\" It is a way for the reader to identify with her and be drawn into the story. A clear conclusion would make it easier to except the end. As humans we want to know, we need to know. Leaving it up to our own imaginations allows us to think of all the possibilities, the good and the bad.<br /><br />Katniss is a tragic hero from the beginning.I recommend reading this book. B000Y8GFY8|5.0|Goldfrapp's \\"Seventh Tree\\" is a departure from anything you have ever heard from them in the past. In fact, I think this album would have been more appropriate to call \\"Supernature\\" than \\"Supernature\\" itself, because this album conveys itself in a very natural and fresh manner. To break this one down track-by-track:<br />1. Clowns: It's amazing that I would find a song so beautiful contains the line, \\"titties that go on and on...\\" and yet, the song is overwhelmingly tranquil. The lyrics initially puzzled me very much. Did she really write such a beautiful song about...tits? I think the answer is yes, but I am sure that was part of the appeal and intent. This song can be analyzed in so many ways, but I'll save that for someone else. This is a fantastic intro to the album because it really does set this mellow, laid-back tone for the rest of the tracks to follow. 10/10<br /><br />2. Little Bird: Not sure what to think of this track. It's really ambiguous and ultimately I don't know what's going on or how to feel after the song ends. It just feels all discombobulated. It's my least favorite on the whole album, but it's still a good song. 6/10<br /><br />3. Happiness: Now momentum is finally picking up in \\"Happiness\\", and may be the most uptempo song on the album. It's a cute little song that seems to be about cults. The lyrics are actually quite dark, and shine a light on the tricks and manipulation groups will use to gain something (money, power, etc.) knowing the person the are preying on is weak or naive. I really enjoyed this song, it's catchy! 9/10<br /><br />4. Road to Somewhere: First couple seconds sound like a slowed-down version of A&E. This is a song where minimalism works really well. Every single sound plays an important or necessary part. It gives you this \\"floating\\" feeling too. The chorus is the highlight of the track, especially for Alison's beautiful vocals. 9/10<br /><br />5. Eat Yourself: Almost reminds me of an early Jewel track, in particular, \\"You Were Meant for Me\\". Again, like Clowns, it contains some bizarre lyrics (\\"If you don't eat yourself, you will explode instead\\"). Still, I think this song is one of the best on the album. It's one of those songs that will put you to sleep when you are too troubled to sleep. It lays down your troubles and puts them to rest. Absolutely lovely and one of my favorites. 10/10<br /><br />6. Some People: It may be at this point you get tired of the slow pace of the album. This is kind of like a slowed-down version of \\"Happiness\\", both the arrangement and lyrics. Except in this song, the protagonist either realizes the ill intent of these bad people, or she/he shrugs it off. I feel like this is auto-biographical in some aspects. I think it was a poor decision to put this track here, after so many slow songs, because it makes it feel like it's dragging. Putting this early in the album would've been smarter. It's a good song, nonetheless. 7/10<br /><br />7. A&E: Again, things start to pick up a little with A&E. This song breaks my heart a little, and is a prime example of Goldfrapp's amazing lyrics. It's the most commercial song on the album, yet they didn't sacrifice substance for a hit. The pace is perfect and the organic arrangement is immediately addicting. 10/10<br /><br />8. Cologne Cerrone Houdini: Alison's vocals, as well as the arrangement, are downright seductive. Lyrics are simple, yet they stick in my head (\\"I'm not your kind; I'm not your girl; See I'm in your car; But not your life\\"). Oddly enough, I listened to this while I was driving, it seemed like she was saying that specific line directly to me! This track is a stand-out, and if you're hesitant to listening to this album, it should be the first or second track you listen to. 10/10<br /><br />9. Caravan Girl: The album's most uptempo song. It's a good song, but I don't have much to say about this song. I think people who enjoyed Supernature/Black Cherry will enjoy this, because it's like the standout tracks on those album, except it's less synth, and more piano/guitar/choir. 9/10<br /><br />10. Monster Love: A good song to tie the album altogether and conclude it. I think the lyrics sum this up quite well: \\"Everything comes around, bringing us back again, here is where we start, and where we end\\". You could almost take this as an answer to the question why they took such a drastically different direction for Seventh Tree after Black Cherry and Supernature. 8/10<br /><br />I am thrilled with the approach they took on this album. How many mainstream bands would do what Goldfrapp did? Overall, the album is beautiful, and equally as impressive as it is expressive. Allison's vocals are the highlight of the album, they fit this laid-back, nature-like music so well! I highly recommend buying the Deluxe edition, it's beautifully packaged and the poster, postcards, and notebook contained are all very stylish and elegantly constructed.

B0011FXLCC|5.0|Goldfrapp's \\"Seventh Tree\\" is a departure from anything you have ever heard from them in the past. In fact, I think this album would have been more appropriate to call \\"Supernature\\" than \\"Supernature\\" itself, because this album conveys itself in a very natural and fresh manner. To break this one down track-by-track:<br />1. Clowns: It's amazing that I would find a song so beautiful contains the line, \\"titties that go on and on...\\" and yet, the song is overwhelmingly tranquil. The lyrics initially puzzled me very much. Did she really write such a beautiful song about...tits? I think the answer is yes, but I am sure that was part of the appeal and intent. This song can be analyzed in so many ways, but I'll save that for someone else. This is a fantastic intro to the album because it really does set this mellow, laid-back tone for the rest of the tracks to follow. 10/10<br /><br />2. Little Bird: Not sure what to think of this track. It's really ambiguous and ultimately I don't know what's going on or how to feel after the song ends. It just feels all discombobulated. It's my least favorite on the whole album, but it's still a good song. 6/10<br /><br />3. Happiness: Now momentum is finally picking up in \\"Happiness\\", and may be the most uptempo song on the album. It's a cute little song that seems to be about cults. The lyrics are actually quite dark, and shine a light on the tricks and manipulation groups will use to gain something (money, power, etc.) knowing the person the are preying on is weak or naive. I really enjoyed this song, it's catchy! 9/10<br /><br />4. Road to Somewhere: First couple seconds sound like a slowed-down version of A&E. This is a song where minimalism works really well. Every single sound plays an important or necessary part. It gives you this \\"floating\\" feeling too. The chorus is the highlight of the track, especially for Alison's beautiful vocals. 9/10<br /><br />5. Eat Yourself: Almost reminds me of an early Jewel track, in particular, \\"You Were Meant for Me\\". Again, like Clowns, it contains some bizarre lyrics (\\"If you don't eat yourself, you will explode instead\\"). Still, I think this song is one of the best on the album. It's one of those songs that will put you to sleep when you are too troubled to sleep. It lays down your troubles and puts them to rest. Absolutely lovely and one of my favorites. 10/10<br /><br />6. Some People: It may be at this point you get tired of the slow pace of the album. This is kind of like a slowed-down version of \\"Happiness\\", both the arrangement and lyrics. Except in this song, the protagonist either realizes the ill intent of these bad people, or she/he shrugs it off. I feel like this is auto-biographical in some aspects. I think it was a poor decision to put this track here, after so many slow songs, because it makes it feel like it's dragging. Putting this early in the album would've been smarter. It's a good song, nonetheless. 7/10<br /><br />7. A&E: Again, things start to pick up a little with A&E. This song breaks my heart a little, and is a prime example of Goldfrapp's amazing lyrics. It's the most commercial song on the album, yet they didn't sacrifice substance for a hit. The pace is perfect and the organic arrangement is immediately addicting. 10/10<br /><br />8. Cologne Cerrone Houdini: Alison's vocals, as well as the arrangement, are downright seductive. Lyrics are simple, yet they stick in my head (\\"I'm not your kind; I'm not your girl; See I'm in your car; But not your life\\"). Oddly enough, I listened to this while I was driving, it seemed like she was saying that specific line directly to me! This track is a stand-out, and if you're hesitant to listening to this album, it should be the first or second track you listen to. 10/10<br /><br />9. Caravan Girl: The album's most uptempo song. It's a good song, but I don't have much to say about this song. I think people who enjoyed Supernature/Black Cherry will enjoy this, because it's like the standout tracks on those album, except it's less synth, and more piano/guitar/choir. 9/10<br /><br />10. Monster Love: A good song to tie the album altogether and conclude it. I think the lyrics sum this up quite well: \\"Everything comes around, bringing us back again, here is where we start, and where we end\\". You could almost take this as an answer to the question why they took such a drastically different direction for Seventh Tree after Black Cherry and Supernature. 8/10<br /><br />I am thrilled with the approach they took on this album. How many mainstream bands would do what Goldfrapp did? Overall, the album is beautiful, and equally as impressive as it is expressive. Allison's vocals are the highlight of the album, they fit this laid-back, nature-like music so well! I highly recommend buying the Deluxe edition, it's beautifully packaged and the poster, postcards, and notebook contained are all very stylish and elegantly constructed.

I don't recall which one it was, but it was about a trial and the lawyers, as always. I promised to myself, since I was a kid at the time (still am), that I would read his adult novels when I grew up someday. A few years later, the first Theodore Boone novel came out, and I, as a kid, was instantly drawn to it. I thought, perhaps I wouldn't have to wait that long to read a Grisham novel! And then I read it, and it ended on a cliffhanger, so of course I wanted the second one. When the second one came out, I raced to the library. I read it. And I was so, so, disappointed.<br /><br />Now, I do not claim to know John Grisham's works very well, since, as I stated before, I am a teenager. However, I do know enough that John Grisham is apparently the master of the legal thriller. After reading these three novels in the Theodore Boone series, I have trouble believing that.<br /><br />Why? Well, the first one was fine. It was short and kind of incomplete, but it had some nice Law-and-Order style drama. Why was I disappointed by the second one? Because it had nothing to do with the first one. This third book in the series faces the same problem.<br /><br />The second one focused on a kidnapping. This one focuses on a robbery. But that isn't the reason why I'm so annoyed. The reason is, HE IS NOT FOCUSING ON THE PETE DUFFY MURDER TRIAL. Sure, in the beginning of the book, there's a bit of tension with him. But that's it. Like in The Abduction, Duffy is pushed off to the side and completely forgotten about.<br /><br />Mr. Grisham has to realize that what draws people to his books are the courtroom aspect of it. Perhaps he thinks that with young children, who are the people this book is marketed to, he can get away with delaying it, putting it off; perhaps he even thinks this ramps up the suspense. But it's not working. Just think of it this way: if you heard about Theodore Boone: Kid Lawyer and knew you would have to wait two, possibly three more years for the conclusion to it, would you want to read it? If you knew, the author was going to write filler novel after filler novel in the series, not advancing the plot he started in the first book, would you want to read it?<br /><br />I'll be counting how many books it takes for Mr. Grisham to return to what I started reading Theodore Boone for. I'll be counting how many books it takes for him to realize that changing the subject pointlessly in this series is not what we want. I'll be counting how many books it takes for him to write about the darned Pete Duffy murder trial again. I'll be counting.<br /><br />-reviewed by a teenager. (I apologize for any teenagery and/or snarky comments in this review, if they have offended you. I understand they can be very annoying, just like teenagers themselves. In any case, thanks for dealing with them and thanks for reading this review.)ize that what draws people to his books are the courtroom aspect of it. Perhaps he thinks that with young children, who are the people this book is marketed to, he can get away with delaying it, putting it off; perhaps he even thinks this ramps up the suspense. But it's not working. Just think of it this way: if you heard about Theodore Boone: Kid Lawyer and knew you would have to wait two, possibly three more years for the conclusion to it, would you want to read it? If you knew, the author was going to write filler novel after filler novel in the series, not advancing the plot he started in the first book, would you want to read it?<br /><br />I'll be counting how many books it takes for Mr. Grisham to return to what I started reading Theodore Boone for. I'll be counting how many books it takes for him to realize that changing the subject pointlessly in this series is not what we want. I'll be counting how many books it takes for him to write about the darned Pete Duffy murder trial again. I'll be counting.<br /><br />-reviewed by a teenager. (I apologize for any teenagery and/or snarky comments in this review, if they have offended you. I understand they can be very annoying, just like teenagers themselves. In any case, thanks for dealing with them and thanks for reading this review.)

1400069289|4.0|Habits can make us or break us.  We are definitely creatures of habit, from an evolutionary point of view and practically speaking.  We need habits to conserve mental energy and to be efficient.  The cliche example is now the number of variations to get dressed in the morning if you did not use habits - it's up in the millions (some people dress as if they've tried option 1.23 million).<br /><br />Habits create grooves in our brain that reinforce them.  The author explains the three components of a habit - the craving, the routine and the reward.  All three need to be taken into account when attempting to change a habit.  You need to become your own personal \\"habitologist\\" to first identify each of the three components and then potential substitutes for each, until you find the triad that works for you.  For example, if you find yourself stuffing your face with a coffee and donuts every afternoon, steadily gaining weight as you approach Michelin Man proportions, what specifically constitutes your habit?  Is the reward a soothing of hunger or is it stress release, when exactly does the habit occur, what is the craving you feel?  This analysis will help you replace a bad habit with a good one.<br /><br />The author also explains how organizational habits can be identified and harnessed to effect positive change.  He gives as an example Paul O'Neill's focus on safety when he took over the reigns of Alcoa.  By developing good habits around safety, claims Duhigg, other good habits also develop, in this case habits like offering suggestions for improved processes.  I don't know that I buy this entirely.  Perhaps its not the habit but the culture, which is a collection of many more things than just habits, that leads to better habits.  The same concern applies to his explanation of the success of the Birmingham bus boycott to social habits - the support of community for Rosa Parks because of her weak ties to many other people in Birmingham.  It seems to me that this castsaside the courage involved as well as the spiritual strengths that made the boycott a success.  Perhaps those social habits played a part, but, in my opinion, that part was small.<br /><br />Overall however, the book is great explanation of how habits are formed and how they can be changed.

0340896965|5.0|Weaving culture, recent history and romance with a truly skillful hand, author David Nicholls creates a tapestry of modern life filled with contrasting colors and characters so real you'll be sure you've sat next to them in a coffee shop. The snapshot approach where each chapter covers the same date through passing years works very naturally, each scene's end nicely tied to the next and backstory perfectly viewed through the characters' eyes. There's a really intriguing blend of messages in this novel too, carpe diem contrasting with persistence and patience, while success too cheaply offered is compared to success denied.<br /><br />Emma puts off both her dreams and her feelings for Dex, while Dex puts off any kind of hard work or commitment. Both settle, as real people so often do, for second bests and expediency. Both grow and change very believably through the years. And both somehow remain true to their hopes and their dreams.<br /><br />One Day is a surprisingly real, enthralling, hopeful, sad and humorous tale--like life painted in colors that almost make sense, the different threads all blending into something beautiful. There's a quintessential Englishness to time and place, great description, superb dialog, and an unsparing look at the trials and joys of modern life. Altogether it's a thoroughly good read and really hard to put down.<br /><br />Disclosure: My husband gave me this book for mothers' day. He has the knack of knowing just what I'll enjoy reading.

1439156816|4.0|Imagine yourself sitting in a bar in Boston watching a Red Sox game on television. The batter hits a hard shot up the middle and Dustin Pedroia nabs it on the bounce going away, flips the ball backwards from his glove to the shortstop to nail the lead runner. The middle-aged guy sitting three seats down from you nods.<br /><br />\\"That's the way I would have done it,\\" he says.<br /><br />Unless the guy's name is Rick Burleson, you are struck by the unmitigated gall of this bozo claiming to have the reflexes, sharpness of vision, timing, experience, and skill to replicate an act of athletic ballet that perhaps fewer than one person in a million could do.<br /><br />I find myself in the position of the blowhard in the bar reviewing what Mr. King has to say about writing. If I agree with him, it means I've advanced in my own writing part way along the path he's traveled. If I disagree, well, ....<br /><br />The first section of the book, \\"C.V.\\", is a memoir of King's life, his first attempts at writing, his early life, family, problems; in other words, the life experience that he brought to his writing and what made him a writer.<br /><br />He moves to \\"Toolbox\\" and writes about vocabulary, grammar and elements of style (and not in a stuffy English-class way). He recommends Strunk and White's \\"The Elements of Style\\", a book I've bought and given away, bought and loaned out, et al, to people I knew who wanted to make their writing better. His suggestions are pithy and useful, with concrete examples: use active, not passive voice, used 'said' instead of the multiple weaker alternatives, and removing adverbs, for example. His advice is human-scale; he admits to the inability to kill off all adverbs. His stated goal is to make competent writers good. He doesn't believe anything can make a writer great who isn't great already.<br /><br />He gets further into the meat of the book in \\"On Writing\\", telling what works for him: write a first draft as quickly (adverb!) as you can,let it cool for six weeks before doing your second draft (it'll give you fresh eyes when you go back), then seeking advice from competent readers. He stresses that writers need to read and write a lot; this is a craft, according to the subtitle of the book, and writers need to see what others do and practice.<br /><br />He also covers description (too little? too much? - a judgment call), dialogue, character, symbolism (if you find it in your book when re-reading the first draft, go with it), theme, pacing, research, writing classes and seminars (don't bother), getting an agent, and correct formatting. He gives some good examples. I disagreed with one item and that's only because the book is ten years old. He recommends \\"Writer's Digest\\", but since the internet has exploded since this was written, I'd recommend duotrope.com. It's more current and, I've heard, some of the editors that publishing houses say you should send your manuscript to are spurious names for their slush piles.<br /><br />I read that writers fall into two categories, the plotters (those that plot their books) vs. the pantsers (those that fly by the seat of their pants). King is firmly in the pants category. He believes that plot arrives from situations and attempts to force the story into a plot smothers the story.<br /><br />King devotes a section, \\"On Living: a Postscript\\", to his near-fatal accident, when he was hit by a van. The last two sections are a details example of the difference between his first and second drafts of \\"1408\\", and what he had been reading.<br /><br />This isn't a dry textbook, it's a fully realized story. The text on the back cover refers to it as 'friendly' and I agree. Picture a chance to sit at the kitchen table with Uncle Stevie over coffee and have him regale you with anecdotes, advice, stories and how-to examples, with family members dropping by. If you don't write and don't plan to, it's still worth the read. If you do, this will up your game. King is funny and has an ear for the telling phrase. He refers to bad writing as \\"shoveling s*** from a sitting position\\", says of himself \\"I was built with a love of the night and the unquiet coffin\\", and refers to people who learned to write in prison as \\"doing time in some of America's finest crossbar hotels.\\"<br /><br />I should note I'm prejudiced toward Mr. King. My wife and I chatted with him for a few minutes on the Mass Pike many odd years ago.<br /><br />Recommended, 4 stars.

B005BDZN62|5.0|West Side Story is absolutely a classic musical. A retelling of Romeo and Juliet set in 1950s New York City, I was singing along to many of the songs without much trouble at all. They are well known songs, found in so much of our modern culture. Seeing this movie is an absolute must in order to understand a wealth of references in books, movies, and TV shows.<br /><br />There are so many aspects to the story that stand out. The \\"local\\" gang is against the outsider Puerto Ricans - but it turns out that the locals are actually made up of Irish, Polish, and other immigrants which only a generation ago would have been the outsiders. Where most movies of this nature would be all-guys-all-the-time, the women in the story are as just as powerful, working in the sweatshop, striving to form a new life where their independence is valued.<br /><br />Where other musicals of the time could be sickly-sweet, West Side Story tackles some important issues head-on. The characters lay out arguments about why they are misunderstood - and then turn the argument around to look at it from all angles. Amazingly, there's even a near-gang-rape of one of the female characters, showing how powerful the \\"not-us\\" mentality could get.<br /><br />This definitely is a movie that you could watch repeatedly and see new subtleties in it each time. The way the characters relate to each other, how they change over time, and how they either listen to or ignore influences from others is well done.<br /><br />It's surprising to me that for many of these actors this was their one shining moment on the screen. I would have thought many of these individuals would go on to star in numerous other movies. This seemed to have become a \\"perfect moment\\" where the cast worked well together.<br /><br />If I had to look for one thing to single out as being less-than-perfect, it would be Russ Tamblyn's dubbed singing in his intro song. B000022TSY|5.0|What an interesting movie. Apparently Easy Rider gets its primary kudos, and placement on the AFI top 100 list at spot 88, for being a fully independent movie. It was one of the first to really make inroads in the high-power movie system. Dennis Hopper and Peter Fonda pretty much made this movie on their own, funded it, and then filmed themselves riding motorcycles across the US.<br /><br />The motorcycles are now iconic, although one has to wonder with their tiny gas tanks just how often the characters would have had to stop for gas on their cross-country trip through some fairly empty wastelands.<br /><br />Much of the movie is full of the gorgeous scenery of the United States. Having driven many of these roads myself, I found myself remembering those trips fondly while watching the movie, and wishing I could go back and take those trips again. The beauty of Monument Valley and other locations is brought vividly to life. In many ways it's a celebration of the wild range of landscapes.<br /><br />There are also a variety of cultures shown here, which is equally interesting. Yes, we get the hippie commune of \\"city-folk\\" men and women trying their best to live off the dry, dusty land. We see small-town deep south, where the teen girls are intrigued by the strangers and the men-folk are threatened. There are the prostitutes of New Orleans, the quiet farmer and his family, and more. Everyone has their own view on life and what it means to live.<br /><br />This is definitely a \\"guy's film\\". Nearly all women who appear are nameless and are there to ogle or idolize the men. The only two women who seem to talk more than a word are two are the two prostitutes in New Orleans, who go on a long acid trip with the guys. Most of their \\"talk\\", though, is wild ramblings due to the drugs.<br /><br />Some say that this is a 60s ode to the glory of drugs. I don't see that. If anything, it seems to show that their path of freedom didn't lead to joy. One of the characters sighs, near the end, that \\"we blew it.\\" He praised the farmer who had a wife, a family, and a quiet homestead where he was able to live off the grid. That is praised as being of value. The commune is poked at for being unrealistic - they plant seed into dry sand. The LSD trip, far from being shown as fun and color-filled, is shown as scary and dangerous.<br /><br />A great movie to see at least once, since there are so many other movies, TV shows, and novels that reference scenes in this and expect watchers to be familiar with what is going on. It's also a powerful glimpse into time, to see the forces that were at work in the US in the late 60s.he end, that \\"we blew it.\\" He praised the farmer who had a wife, a family, and a quiet homestead where he was able to live off the grid. 0871404133|3.0|I'm not a biologist, but I have taken an amateur interest in this subject for 20+ years. So I was able to follow the discussions in the book, but they ended up leaving me confused. I followed this up by reading the NATURE article which is referred to several times in the book as being the \\"mathematical proof\\" or the argument he is making.<br /><br />It's just not clear exactly what Wilson is doing, but I think it's essentially a bait-and-switch. He takes a very narrow definition of \\"eusocial\\" and \\"inclusive fitness\\" and argues that inclusive fitness is not the best predictor of eusocial behavior. Then he suddenly makes sweeping conclusions about all of social behavior and altruism, claiming that inclusive fitness explains none of it. This is like saying: \\"Sheila is not a man, therefore no men exist.\\" Um, what???<br /><br />I think that in general the concept behind inclusive fitness (which is to say that if we help out our relatives then we help spread our own genes, because our relatives also share our genes) is not only valid but necessary to explain why social species (such as humans) can be the product of natural selection. And this book doesn't disprove that! Wilson has been sidetracked by a particular question about how certain types of bees evolved to be eusocial and lost sight of the big picture.<br /><br />Furthermore, he keeps talking about how he \\"mathematically proved\\" that inclusive fitness doesn't work, but I just read his NATURE paper and I sure wasn't convinced of that. Nor were the literally hundreds of other authors who wrote replies to his NATURE article, most of which said essentially the same thing that I just said in this review.<br /><br />All that having been said, the book is still a very interesting and useful description of how social behavior interacts with natural selection. If he hadn't spent so much time trying to draw what seemed to be a meaningless distinction between inclusive fitness and what he dubs the \\"standard model\\" of group selection, then it would have been a more understandable and useful book for the non-specialist reader.<br /><br />I will also note that toward the end of the book he goes on some wild tangents, such as why religion is an outgrowth of the evolutionary pressures of social living. I think he nails this perfectly (although he isn't the first to do so), but he knows this explanation is almost certain to be instantly rejected by religious readers. So I'm not sure why he bothered to include it in the book.<br /><br />He also talks a bit about environmentalism and even his assertion that we should give up wasting money on manned space exploration because robot probes could do better space science. I think he fears the idea that we may feel like we can just use up the resources of the Earth and move away to somewhere else, so he wants to stop all such activity. (But if we are never going to leave the Earth, why bother sending out the robot probes anyway?)<br /><br />All in all it is a book with a lot of really good information in it, as well as some very interesting speculation. But the speculation and the information are just a bit too muddled together, making it hard to be sure where one ends and the other begins. This is a serious flaw in a science book.

B007RX32TY|5.0|Chromatics might sound like a color scheme to most, but if you've watched cool-headed, stuntman turned hero film Drive, then there's no doubt that your ears have experienced the smooth, analog-textured synth pop of Portland's very own. With their most recent 90-minute release, Kill For Love, Chromatics have soared into the indie scene as one of the leaders of the new Italo disco movement, formulating a sound combining vintage 80s synthesizers and arpeggiators layered with crisp guitar melodies and backing bass. With a heavy presence of reverb, the dreamy yet chilling voice of Ruth Radelet, and a constant loop of a classic crackle and pop from an old vinyl, Kill For Love is their most visionary effort to date. While tracks such as \\"The Page\\" and \\"Back from the Grave\\" standout as the most poppy and major-chord oriented, it's the 8-minute instrumental, \\"Broken Mirrors,\\" that loses you in its cryptic, steadily rising mix of single note strings and thickly stroked guitar sections.
